do_single_rollout

Problem: To promote her school's annual Kite Olympics, Genevieve makes a small kite and a large kite for a bulletin board display. For her small kite, Genevieve draws the kite on a grid with the points one inch apart, as shown below. [asy] for ( int x = 0; x <= 6; ++x ) { for ( int y = 0; y <= 7; ++y ) { dot((x,y)); } } draw((0,5)--(3,7)--(6,5)--(3,0)--cycle); [/asy] For the large kite she triples both the height and width of the entire grid. What is the number of square inches in the area of the small kite? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{21}

Reference Answer: The kite can be divided into two triangles, each with base 7 and altitude 3. Each area is $(1/2)(7)(3) = 10.5$, so the total area is $2(10.5) = \boxed{21}$ square inches.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✓, Reward: 1.00

do_single_rollout

Problem: When Marika bought her house she paid $80\%$ of the purchase price of the house with a loan. She paid the remaining $\$49,400$ of the purchase price with her savings. What was the purchase price of her house? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{62,500}

Reference Answer: Since she paid $80\%$ with loans, and the rest she paid with her savings, $20\%$ of the purchase price is what she paid with her savings. Thus, if we call $x$ the total price of the house, we have: $\frac{x}{5} = 49400 \rightarrow x = \boxed{247000}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: In convex quadrilateral $ABCD$, $AB=BC=13$, $CD=DA=24$, and $\angle D=60^\circ$. Points $X$ and $Y$ are the midpoints of $\overline{BC}$ and $\overline{DA}$ respectively. Compute $XY^2$ (the square of the length of $XY$). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{52}

Reference Answer: We begin by drawing a diagram: [asy] pair A,B,C,D,X,Y,H; A=(-12,12*sqrt(3)); D=(0,0); C=(12,12*sqrt(3)); B=(0,5+12*sqrt(3)); X=(B+C)/2; Y=(A+D)/2; H=(A+C)/2; draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(X--Y); label("$A$",A,W); label("$B$",B,N); label("$C$",C,E); label("$D$",D,S); label("$X$",X,NE); label("$Y$",Y,SW); label("$24$",D--C,SE); label("$13$",A--B,NW); label("$60^\circ$",(0,4)); draw(B--D,heavycyan); draw(A--C,heavycyan); label("$H$",H,NW); [/asy] We draw diagonals $\overline{AC}$ and $\overline{BD}$ and let the intersection point be $H$. Since $\angle ADC=60^\circ$ and $AD=CD$, $\triangle ACD$ is equilateral, so $AC=24$. Since $ABCD$ has two pairs of equal sides, it is a kite, and so its diagonals are perpendicular and $\overline{BD}$ bisects $\overline{AC}$. Thus, \[AH=HC=24/2=12.\]Applying the Pythagorean Theorem on $\triangle BHC$ and $\triangle CHD$ gives \[BH=\sqrt{BC^2-HC^2}=\sqrt{13^2-12^2}=5\]and \[HD=\sqrt{CD^2-HC^2}=\sqrt{24^2-12^2}=12\sqrt{3}.\][asy] size(180); pair A,B,C,D,X,Y,H; A=(-12,12*sqrt(3)); D=(0,0); C=(12,12*sqrt(3)); B=(0,5+12*sqrt(3)); X=(B+C)/2; Y=(A+D)/2; H=(A+C)/2; draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(X--Y); label("$A$",A,W); label("$B$",B,N); label("$C$",C,E); label("$D$",D,S); label("$X$",X,NE); label("$Y$",Y,SW); draw(B--D,heavycyan); draw(A--C,heavycyan); label("$H$",H,NW); pair W; W = (C+D)/2; draw(X--W--Y,dashed); label("$Y'$",W,SE); draw(rightanglemark(B,H,C,20),heavycyan); [/asy] Let $Y'$ be the midpoint of $\overline{CD}$. We look at triangle $BCD$. Since segment $\overline{XY'}$ connects midpoints $X$ and $Y'$, it is parallel to $\overline{BD}$ and has half the length of $\overline{BD}$. Thus, \[XY' = \frac{1}{2}(BH+HD)=\frac{1}{2}(5+12\sqrt{3}).\]Now, we look at triangle $ACD$. Similarly, since $Y$ and $Y'$ are midpoints, $\overline{YY'}$ is parallel to $\overline{AC}$ and has half the length of $\overline{AC}$, so \[YY' = 24/2=12.\]Since $\overline{BD} \perp \overline{AC}$, we have $\overline{XY'}\perp \overline{YY'}$, so $\angle XY'Y=90^\circ$. Finally, we use the Pythagorean theorem on $\triangle XY'Y$ to compute \begin{align*} XY^2=YY'^2+XY'^2&=12^2+\left(\frac{1}{2}(5+12\sqrt{3})\right)^2\\ &=144+\frac{1}{4}(25+120\sqrt{3}+144\cdot 3) \\ &= \boxed{\frac{1033}{4}+30\sqrt{3}}. \end{align*}

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Reference Answer: We begin by drawing a diagram: [asy] pair A,B,C,D,X,Y,H; A=(-12,12*sqrt(3)); D=(0,0); C=(12,12*sqrt(3)); B=(0,5+12*sqrt(3)); X=(B+C)/2; Y=(A+D)/2; H=(A+C)/2; draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(X--Y); label("$A$",A,W); label("$B$",B,N); label("$C$",C,E); label("$D$",D,S); label("$X$",X,NE); label("$Y$",Y,SW); label("$24$",D--C,SE); label("$13$",A--B,NW); label("$60^\circ$",(0,4)); draw(B--D,heavycyan); draw(A--C,heavycyan); label("$H$",H,NW); [/asy] We draw diagonals $\overline{AC}$ and $\overline{BD}$ and let the intersection point be $H$. Since $\angle ADC=60^\circ$ and $AD=CD$, $\triangle ACD$ is equilateral, so $AC=24$. Since $ABCD$ has two pairs of equal sides, it is a kite, and so its diagonals are perpendicular and $\overline{BD}$ bisects $\overline{AC}$. Thus, \[AH=HC=24/2=12.\]Applying the Pythagorean Theorem on $\triangle BHC$ and $\triangle CHD$ gives \[BH=\sqrt{BC^2-HC^2}=\sqrt{13^2-12^2}=5\]and \[HD=\sqrt{CD^2-HC^2}=\sqrt{24^2-12^2}=12\sqrt{3}.\][asy] size(180); pair A,B,C,D,X,Y,H; A=(-12,12*sqrt(3)); D=(0,0); C=(12,12*sqrt(3)); B=(0,5+12*sqrt(3)); X=(B+C)/2; Y=(A+D)/2; H=(A+C)/2; draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(X--Y); label("$A$",A,W); label("$B$",B,N); label("$C$",C,E); label("$D$",D,S); label("$X$",X,NE); label("$Y$",Y,SW); draw(B--D,heavycyan); draw(A--C,heavycyan); label("$H$",H,NW); pair W; W = (C+D)/2; draw(X--W--Y,dashed); label("$Y'$",W,SE); draw(rightanglemark(B,H,C,20),heavycyan); [/asy] Let $Y'$ be the midpoint of $\overline{CD}$. We look at triangle $BCD$. Since segment $\overline{XY'}$ connects midpoints $X$ and $Y'$, it is parallel to $\overline{BD}$ and has half the length of $\overline{BD}$. Thus, \[XY' = \frac{1}{2}(BH+HD)=\frac{1}{2}(5+12\sqrt{3}).\]Now, we look at triangle $ACD$. Similarly, since $Y$ and $Y'$ are midpoints, $\overline{YY'}$ is parallel to $\overline{AC}$ and has half the length of $\overline{AC}$, so \[YY' = 24/2=12.\]Since $\overline{BD} \perp \overline{AC}$, we have $\overline{XY'}\perp \overline{YY'}$, so $\angle XY'Y=90^\circ$. Finally, we use the Pythagorean theorem on $\triangle XY'Y$ to compute \begin{align*} XY^2=YY'^2+XY'^2&=12^2+\left(\frac{1}{2}(5+12\sqrt{3})\right)^2\\ &=144+\frac{1}{4}(25+120\sqrt{3}+144\cdot 3) \\ &= \boxed{\frac{1033}{4}+30\sqrt{3}}. \end{align*}

Reference Answer: We begin by drawing a diagram: [asy] pair A,B,C,D,X,Y,H; A=(-12,12*sqrt(3)); D=(0,0); C=(12,12*sqrt(3)); B=(0,5+12*sqrt(3)); X=(B+C)/2; Y=(A+D)/2; H=(A+C)/2; draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(X--Y); label("$A$",A,W); label("$B$",B,N); label("$C$",C,E); label("$D$",D,S); label("$X$",X,NE); label("$Y$",Y,SW); label("$24$",D--C,SE); label("$13$",A--B,NW); label("$60^\circ$",(0,4)); draw(B--D,heavycyan); draw(A--C,heavycyan); label("$H$",H,NW); [/asy] We draw diagonals $\overline{AC}$ and $\overline{BD}$ and let the intersection point be $H$. Therefore, the radius is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. **Final Answer** The radius of the circle is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. </think> To determine the radius of the circle onto which the bilinear transformation \( f(z) = \frac{i z + 2}{4z + i} \) maps the real and imaginary axes, we analyze the images of these axes under the transformation. ### Image of the Real Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The real axis can be parametrized as \( z = t \) where \( t \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = t \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(t) = \frac{i t + 2}{4 t + i} \] 3. **Separating Real and Imaginary Parts**: \[ f(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1} + i \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] This gives the parametric equations: \[ u(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1}, \quad v(t) = \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] 4. **Finding the Circle**: - By eliminating the parameter \( t \), we find that the image lies on a circle. - The circle's center is found to be \( (0, -\frac{7}{8}) \) and the radius is calculated as \( \frac{9}{8} \). ### Image of the Imaginary Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The imaginary axis can be parametrized as \( z = is \) where \( s \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = is \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(is) = \frac{-s + 2}{i(4s + 1)} \] 3. **Simplifying**: - This results in purely imaginary numbers, indicating the image is a line (the imaginary axis in the complex plane). ### Conclusion The real axis is mapped to a circle with radius \( \frac{9}{8} \). The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

The general method for finding the image of a line under a Möbius transformation is to take three points on the original line, find their images, and then determine the circle or line through those points. Let's apply this method to the imaginary axis. Take three points on the imaginary axis: 1. z = 0: f(0) = 2/i = -2i. 2. z = i: f(i) = (i*i + 2)/(4*i + i) = (-1 + 2)/(5i) = 1/(5i) = -i/5. 3. z = ∞: The transformation as z approaches ∞ along the imaginary axis: f(z) = (i*z + 2)/(4*z + i) ≈ (i*z)/(4*z) = i/4. So the three image points are -2i, -i/5, and i/4. These three points are on the imaginary axis. To determine if they lie on a circle, note that three colinear points (on the same line) lie on an infinite number of circles, but in the standard plane, the only circle that includes them is the line itself, considered as a circle with infinite radius. The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. Therefore, the radius is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. **Final Answer** The radius of the circle is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. </think> To determine the radius of the circle onto which the bilinear transformation \( f(z) = \frac{i z + 2}{4z + i} \) maps the real and imaginary axes, we analyze the images of these axes under the transformation. ### Image of the Real Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The real axis can be parametrized as \( z = t \) where \( t \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = t \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(t) = \frac{i t + 2}{4 t + i} \] 3. **Separating Real and Imaginary Parts**: \[ f(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1} + i \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] This gives the parametric equations: \[ u(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1}, \quad v(t) = \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] 4. **Finding the Circle**: - By eliminating the parameter \( t \), we find that the image lies on a circle. - The circle's center is found to be \( (0, -\frac{7}{8}) \) and the radius is calculated as \( \frac{9}{8} \). ### Image of the Imaginary Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The imaginary axis can be parametrized as \( z = is \) where \( s \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = is \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(is) = \frac{-s + 2}{i(4s + 1)} \] 3. **Simplifying**: - This results in purely imaginary numbers, indicating the image is a line (the imaginary axis in the complex plane). ### Conclusion The real axis is mapped to a circle with radius \( \frac{9}{8} \). The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. Therefore, the radius is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. **Final Answer** The radius of the circle is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. </think> To determine the radius of the circle onto which the bilinear transformation \( f(z) = \frac{i z + 2}{4z + i} \) maps the real and imaginary axes, we analyze the images of these axes under the transformation. ### Image of the Real Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The real axis can be parametrized as \( z = t \) where \( t \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = t \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(t) = \frac{i t + 2}{4 t + i} \] 3. **Separating Real and Imaginary Parts**: \[ f(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1} + i \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] This gives the parametric equations: \[ u(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1}, \quad v(t) = \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] 4. **Finding the Circle**: - By eliminating the parameter \( t \), we find that the image lies on a circle. - The circle's center is found to be \( (0, -\frac{7}{8}) \) and the radius is calculated as \( \frac{9}{8} \). ### Image of the Imaginary Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The imaginary axis can be parametrized as \( z = is \) where \( s \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = is \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(is) = \frac{-s + 2}{i(4s + 1)} \] 3. **Simplifying**: - This results in purely imaginary numbers, indicating the image is a line (the imaginary axis in the complex plane). ### Conclusion The real axis is mapped to a circle with radius \( \frac{9}{8} \). The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. Therefore, the radius is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. **Final Answer** The radius of the circle is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. </think> To determine the radius of the circle onto which the bilinear transformation \( f(z) = \frac{i z + 2}{4z + i} \) maps the real and imaginary axes, we analyze the images of these axes under the transformation. ### Image of the Real Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The real axis can be parametrized as \( z = t \) where \( t \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = t \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(t) = \frac{i t + 2}{4 t + i} \] 3. **Separating Real and Imaginary Parts**: \[ f(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1} + i \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] This gives the parametric equations: \[ u(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1}, \quad v(t) = \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] 4. **Finding the Circle**: - By eliminating the parameter \( t \), we find that the image lies on a circle. - The circle's center is found to be \( (0, -\frac{7}{8}) \) and the radius is calculated as \( \frac{9}{8} \). ### Image of the Imaginary Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The imaginary axis can be parametrized as \( z = is \) where \( s \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = is \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(is) = \frac{-s + 2}{i(4s + 1)} \] 3. **Simplifying**: - This results in purely imaginary numbers, indicating the image is a line (the imaginary axis in the complex plane). ### Conclusion The real axis is mapped to a circle with radius \( \frac{9}{8} \). The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

Let me denote the circle equation as \( (x - a)^2 + (y - b)^2 = r^2 \) Plugging in the three points: 1. For (0, -2): \( (0 - a)^2 + (-2 - b)^2 = r^2 \) => \( a^2 + (b + 2)^2 = r^2 \) 2. For (0, 1/4): \( (0 - a)^2 + (1/4 - b)^2 = r^2 \) => \( a^2 + (b - 1/4)^2 = r^2 \) 3. For (9/17, 2/17): \( (9/17 - a)^2 + (2/17 - b)^2 = r^2 \) Subtract equation 2 from equation 1: \( [a^2 + (b + 2)^2] - [a^2 + (b - 1/4)^2] = 0 \) Simplify: \( (b + 2)^2 - (b - 1/4)^2 = 0 \) Expand both squares: \( [b^2 +4b +4] - [b^2 - (1/2)b + 1/16] = 0 \) Simplify: \( b^2 +4b +4 -b^2 + (1/2)b -1/16 = 0 \) Combine like terms: \( (4b + (1/2)b) + (4 - 1/16) = 0 \) \( (9/2)b + (63/16) = 0 \) Multiply both sides by 16 to eliminate denominators: \( 72b + 63 = 0 \) => 72b = -63 => b = -63/72 = -7/8 So, the y-coordinate of the center is -7/8. Now, substitute b = -7/8 into equation 1: \( a^2 + (-7/8 + 2)^2 = r^2 \) Compute -7/8 + 2 = -7/8 + 16/8 = 9/8 So, \( a^2 + (9/8)^2 = r^2 \) => \( a^2 + 81/64 = r^2 \) Similarly, substitute b = -7/8 into equation 2: \( a^2 + (-7/8 - 1/4)^2 = r^2 \) Compute -7/8 - 2/8 = -9/8 Thus, \( a^2 + (-9/8)^2 = r^2 \) => same as above, so no new information. Now, use equation 3 with b = -7/8: \( (9/17 - a)^2 + (2/17 - (-7/8))^2 = r^2 \) First, compute 2/17 - (-7/8) = 2/17 + 7/8 = (16/136 + 119/136) = 135/136 So, \( (9/17 - a)^2 + (135/136)^2 = r^2 \) We also have from equation 1: \( a^2 + 81/64 = r^2 \) Set them equal: \( (9/17 - a)^2 + (135/136)^2 = a^2 + 81/64 \) Expand the left side: \( (81/289 - (18/17)a + a^2) + (18225/18496) = a^2 + 81/64 \) Simplify: \( a^2 - (18/17)a + 81/289 + 18225/18496 = a^2 + 81/64 \) Cancel \( a^2 \): \( - (18/17)a + 81/289 + 18225/18496 = 81/64 \) Multiply all terms by 18496 (which is 17^2 * 16^2) to eliminate denominators: Let's compute each term: - (18/17)a * 18496 = -18 * 18496 /17 * a = -18*1088*a = -19584 a 81/289 *18496 = 81 * (18496 /289) = 81 * 64 = 5184 18225/18496 *18496 = 18225 81/64 *18496 = 81 * 18496 /64 = 81 * 289 = 23409 So the equation becomes: -19584a + 5184 + 18225 = 23409 Compute 5184 + 18225 = 23409 Thus: -19584a + 23409 = 23409 Subtract 23409: -19584a = 0 => a = 0 Therefore, the center is at (0, -7/8) and radius squared is \( a^2 + 81/64 = 81/64 \), so radius is 9/8. Therefore, the image of the real axis is a circle with center at (0, -7/8) and radius 9/8. Wait, but let's check if the point (9/17, 2/17) lies on this circle. Center is (0, -7/8), radius 9/8. The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]

Therefore, the radius is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. **Final Answer** The radius of the circle is \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}}. </think> To determine the radius of the circle onto which the bilinear transformation \( f(z) = \frac{i z + 2}{4z + i} \) maps the real and imaginary axes, we analyze the images of these axes under the transformation. ### Image of the Real Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The real axis can be parametrized as \( z = t \) where \( t \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = t \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(t) = \frac{i t + 2}{4 t + i} \] 3. **Separating Real and Imaginary Parts**: \[ f(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1} + i \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] This gives the parametric equations: \[ u(t) = \frac{9t}{16t^2 + 1}, \quad v(t) = \frac{4t^2 - 2}{16t^2 + 1} \] 4. **Finding the Circle**: - By eliminating the parameter \( t \), we find that the image lies on a circle. - The circle's center is found to be \( (0, -\frac{7}{8}) \) and the radius is calculated as \( \frac{9}{8} \). ### Image of the Imaginary Axis 1. **Parametrization**: The imaginary axis can be parametrized as \( z = is \) where \( s \) is a real number. 2. **Transformation**: Substituting \( z = is \) into \( f(z) \): \[ f(is) = \frac{-s + 2}{i(4s + 1)} \] 3. **Simplifying**: - This results in purely imaginary numbers, indicating the image is a line (the imaginary axis in the complex plane). ### Conclusion The real axis is mapped to a circle with radius \( \frac{9}{8} \). The imaginary axis is mapped to a line, which in the extended complex plane can be considered a circle passing through infinity. However, the problem specifies a finite radius, so we conclude the radius of the circle onto which the real axis is mapped is the answer. \[ \boxed{\dfrac{9}{8}} \]
